The problem says: Two quantum states with energies E1 and E2 have a lifetime of 10^-9 seconds and 10^-8 seconds, respectively. We want to clearly distinguish these two energy levels. The question is asking which energy difference would allow us to clearly resolve these two states. The given correct answer is 10^-8 eV, but I need to understand why that is.

First, let me recall some concepts from quantum mechanics. In quantum mechanics, the energy of a state is related to its lifetime through the uncertainty principle. The Heisenberg uncertainty principle states that the more precisely we know the energy (or the lifetime) of a state, the less precisely we can know the other. Specifically, the energy-time uncertainty relation is given by:

ΔE Δt ≈ ħ/2

Where ΔE is the energy uncertainty, Δt is the time uncertainty (or lifetime), and ħ is the reduced Planck's constant.

Since the problem mentions lifetimes, I think this is related to the uncertainty in the energy levels. If two states have different lifetimes, their energy difference must be such that it's large enough to be resolved, meaning the energy uncertainty should be comparable or larger than the difference in their lifetimes.

Okay, so I have this question about quantum states and their lifetimes. Let me try to make sense of it step by step. The question says that there are two quantum states with energies E1 and E2. These states have lifetimes of 10^-9 seconds and 10^-8 seconds respectively. The goal is to clearly distinguish these two energy levels, and we need to find the correct energy difference between them so that they can be resolved. The answer given is 10^-8 eV, but I need to understand why that is the case.

First, I remember that in quantum mechanics, the energy levels of a system are often associated with their lifetimes through the uncertainty principle. The Heisenberg uncertainty principle states that we cannot precisely know both the energy and the lifetime of a state simultaneously. The more accurately we know the energy, the less accurately we can know the lifetime, and vice versa.

The uncertainty principle in energy and lifetime is given by the formula:

ΔE Δt ≥ ħ/2

Where:
- ΔE is the uncertainty in energy (or the energy difference we are looking for)
- Δt is the uncertainty in lifetime (the inverse of the lifetime of the state)
- ħ is the reduced Planck constant (approximately 1.0545718 × 10^-34 J·s)

So, to clearly distinguish the two states, the energy difference should be such that it's large enough to make their lifetimes distinguishable. That probably means that the difference in their lifetimes should correspond to a measurable change in energy.

Given the lifetimes, τ1 = 10^-9 s and τ2 = 10^-8 s, we can compute the energy uncertainty ΔE needed to resolve them.

Let me write down the values:

τ1 = 10^-9 s  
τ2 = 10^-8 s

So, the difference in lifetimes is Δτ = τ2 - τ1 = 10^-8 s - 10^-9 s = 9 × 10^-10 s.

Now, using the uncertainty principle, ΔE Δτ ≥ ħ/2.

We can solve for ΔE:

ΔE ≥ ħ / (2 Δτ)

Plugging in the numbers:

ΔE ≥ (1.0545718 × 10^-34 J·s) / (2 × 9 × 10^-10 s)

Let me compute that denominator first:

2 × 9 × 10^-10 = 18 × 10^-10 = 1.8 × 10^-9 s

So now:

ΔE ≥ (1.0545718 × 10^-34 J·s) / (1.8 × 10^-9 s)

Calculating this:

10^-34 divided by 10^-9 is 10^-25, and 1.0545718 / 1.8 is approximately 0.588.

So, ΔE ≈ 0.588 × 10^-25 J

But the question asks for the energy difference in electron volts (eV). I remember that 1 eV is equal to 1.602176634 × 10^-19 J.

So, to convert from Joules to eV, we divide by 1.602176634 × 10^-19 J/eV.

Let me do that:

ΔE (eV) = (0.588 × 10^-25 J) / (1.602176634 × 10^-19 J/eV)

Calculating this:

10^-25 divided by 10^-19 is 10^-6, and 0.588 / 1.602176634 is approximately 0.368.

So, ΔE ≈ 0.368 × 10^-6 eV

The formula for the uncertainty principle in energy is ΔE * Δt ≥ ħ/2, where ΔE is the energy difference, Δt is the lifetime, and ħ is the reduced Planck constant.

Okay, so I have this question about quantum states and their lifetimes. It says that two quantum states have energies E1 and E2 with lifetimes of 10^-9 seconds and 10^-8 seconds respectively. We need to determine which energy difference between E2 and E1 would allow us to clearly distinguish these two energy levels. Alright, let's start by recalling some concepts from quantum mechanics. The lifetime of a quantum state is related to its energy through the Heisenberg uncertainty principle. The uncertainty principle states that the more precisely we know the energy of a state, the less precisely we can know its lifetime, and vice versa. The formula that relates them is ΔE * Δt ≈ ħ, where ΔE is the energy uncertainty and Δt is the time uncertainty (or lifetime in this case).

So, in this problem, we have two states with lifetimes Δt1 = 10^-9 seconds and Δt2 = 10^-8 seconds. We want to resolve them, meaning we need their energy difference to be large enough so that their energy levels don't overlap when measured. That is, the energy difference ΔE should be larger than the energy uncertainty associated with each state.

Wait, but actually, to clearly distinguish two states, the energy difference between them should be larger than the natural linewidth of each state. The natural linewidth is related to the lifetime by the uncertainty principle. The linewidth Γ is given by Γ ≈ ħ / Δt. So, to resolve two states, the energy difference ΔE should be larger than Γ1 and Γ2, where Γ1 = ħ / Δt1 and Γ2 = ħ / Δt2.

So, let's compute Γ1 and Γ2.

First, I need to recall the value of ħ (h-bar). ħ is approximately 1.0545718 × 10^-34 Joule-seconds.

But since the answer is given in eV, I should convert Joules to eV because the energy difference is required in eV. 1 eV is equal to 1.602176634 × 10^-19 Joules. So, 1 Joule is 1 / (1.602176634 × 10^-19) eV, which is approximately 6.241509e18 eV.

So, let's compute Γ1:

Γ1 = ħ / Δt1 = (1.0545718 × 10^-34 J·s) / (10^-9 s) = 1.0545718 × 10^-34 / 10^-9 J = 1.0545718 × 10^-35 J.

Convert this to eV:

1.0545718 × 10^-35 J * 6.241509e18 eV/J = approximately 6.607e-17 eV.

Similarly, compute Γ2:

Γ2 = ħ / Δt2 = 1.0545718 × 10^-34 J·s / 10^-8 s = 1.0545718 × 10^-34 / 10^-8 J = 1.0545718 × 10^-26 J.

Convert to eV:

1.0545718 × 10^-26 J * 6.241509e18 eV/J = approximately 6.605e-8 eV.

So, Γ1 is approximately 6.607e-17 eV and Γ2 is approximately 6.605e-8 eV.

First, I remember that in quantum mechanics, the Heisenberg uncertainty principle relates the uncertainty in energy (or equivalently, the energy difference between two states) to the uncertainty in time (or the lifetime of the states). Alright, so I came across this quantum mechanics problem, and I want to figure it out step by step. Let me try to understand what's being asked here.

We have two quantum states with energies E1 and E2. The lifetimes of these states are given as 10^-9 seconds and 10^-8 seconds, respectively. The question is about determining the energy difference between these two states so that they can be clearly distinguished. The correct answer is supposed to be 10^-8 eV, but I need to understand why.

Hmm, okay. So, first off, I recall that in quantum mechanics, the energy levels are quantized, and the lifetime of a state is related to its energy through the uncertainty principle. The uncertainty principle, or Heisenberg's uncertainty principle, states that you can't precisely know both the energy and the lifetime of a state simultaneously. The more precisely you know the energy, the less precisely you can know the lifetime, and vice versa.

So, the Heisenberg uncertainty principle can be expressed as:

ΔE * Δt ≥ ħ / 2

Where ΔE is the uncertainty in energy, Δt is the uncertainty in lifetime, and ħ is the reduced Planck's constant.

But wait, in this problem, we are given the actual lifetimes of the two states, not the uncertainties. So, how does that play into this?

I think the idea is that if we know the lifetimes precisely, we can calculate the minimum energy uncertainty required to resolve the two states. Since the lifetimes are given, we can use them to find the minimum energy difference needed so that the states don't overlap in their energy and lifetime uncertainties.

Let me write that down. The uncertainty principle gives:

ΔE ≥ ħ / (2Δt)

So, if we have two states with lifetimes Δt1 and Δt2, the minimum energy difference required for them to be distinguishable would be based on the smaller of the two lifetimes, right? Because the more precise the lifetime, the larger the required energy difference.

Yes, that makes sense. If one state has a longer lifetime, its energy can be more precisely known, so the other state needs to have a larger energy difference to be distinguished.

In our case, the lifetimes are 10^-9 seconds and 10^-8 seconds. So, the smaller lifetime is 10^-9 seconds, which corresponds to the state with E1. Therefore, the minimum energy difference ΔE should be calculated using the smaller lifetime.

So, let's compute that.

First, let me recall the value of ħ. Planck's constant h is approximately 6.626 x 10^-34 J·s, so ħ is h divided by 2π, which is roughly 1.0545718 x 10^-34 J·s.

But the answer is given in electron volts (eV), so I need to convert ħ into eV·s.

Since 1 eV is equal to 1.602176634 x 10^-19 J, we can find ħ in eV·s:

ħ = 1.0545718 x 10^-34 J·s * (1 eV / 1.602176634 x 10^-19 J) ≈ 6.582 x 10^-25 eV·s.

Okay, so ħ ≈ 6.582 x 10^-25 eV·s.

Now, using the uncertainty principle:

ΔE ≥ ħ / (2Δt)

We use the smaller lifetime, which is 10^-9 seconds:

Δt = 10^-9 s

So, plugging in:

ΔE ≥ (6.582 x 10^-25 eV·s) / (2 * 10^-9 s)

Simplify the denominator: 2 * 10^-9 s = 2e-9 s

So,

ΔE ≈ 6.582 x 10^-25 / 2e-9 = (6.582 / 2) x 10^(-25 + 9) = 3.291 x 10^-16 eV

In spectroscopy, the resolving power is often given as λ / Δλ, where λ is the wavelength and Δλ is the linewidth in wavelength units.

But energy difference E = hc / λ, linewidth ΔE = hc / Δλ.

ΔE ≥ sqrt(ΔE1^2 + ΔE2^2 )

Alright, so I have this problem here about two quantum states with different energies and lifetimes. The question is asking about the energy difference needed to clearly distinguish these two energy levels. Hmm, okay. Let me try to wrap my head around this.

First, let's parse the problem. There are two quantum states: one has energy E1 and the other E2. Their lifetimes are 10^-9 seconds and 10^-8 seconds respectively. The goal is to find the energy difference between them so that they can be clearly resolved. The answer is given as 10^-8 eV, but I need to figure out why that is the case.

Hmm. So, in quantum mechanics, the lifetime of a state is related to its energy uncertainty through the Heisenberg uncertainty principle. That principle states that you can't precisely know both the energy and the lifetime of a state simultaneously. The more precisely you know the energy, the less precisely you can know the lifetime, and vice versa.

Mathematically, the Heisenberg uncertainty principle is often expressed as:

ΔE * Δt ≥ ħ / 2

Where ΔE is the energy uncertainty, Δt is the time uncertainty, and ħ is the reduced Planck constant. So, rearranging this, we get:

ΔE ≥ ħ / (2Δt)

So, the energy uncertainty is inversely proportional to the time uncertainty. That makes sense because if a state has a shorter lifetime, the energy can't be precisely known, and vice versa.

Wait, but in this problem, we have two different states with different lifetimes. So, we need to consider the energy difference between them to be able to resolve them. Resolving them means that their energy levels are distinct enough that they don't overlap or interfere with each other.

So, if the energy difference between E2 and E1 is large enough, then when you try to measure their energies, you can tell them apart. How large should that difference be?

Well, let's think about the lifetimes. The first state has a lifetime of 10^-9 seconds, and the second has 10^-8 seconds. The longer lifetime state (E2) has a smaller uncertainty in energy? Wait, no. Because the uncertainty principle says that if the lifetime is longer, the energy uncertainty is smaller. So, E1 has a shorter lifetime, which means its energy is more uncertain, and E2 has a longer lifetime, so its energy is more precisely known.

Thus, if we want to distinguish E1 and E2, we have to make sure that the energy difference between E1 and E2 is larger than the uncertainty in E1, because E1 has a larger uncertainty. That way, when we measure E2, we can see a difference even considering the uncertainty in E1.

Energy difference (ΔE) = |E2 - E1| ≥ uncertainty in E1 (ΔE1)

Okay, so I have this quantum mechanics problem here, and I need to figure out why the energy difference between two states should be 10^-8 eV. Hmm, let me try to break this down step by step.

First, the problem says we have two quantum states with energies E1 and E2, and their lifetimes are 10^-9 seconds and 10^-8 seconds, respectively. The goal is to clearly distinguish these two energy levels, and the correct answer is given as 10^-8 eV for their energy difference. But why is that?

Alright, so I remember that in quantum mechanics, the uncertainty principle plays a crucial role. The Heisenberg Uncertainty Principle states that we can't precisely know both the energy and the lifetime of a quantum state simultaneously. The more precisely we know the energy, the less precisely we can know the lifetime, and vice versa.

Mathematically, the uncertainty principle is often written as ΔE Δt ≥ ħ/2, where ΔE is the energy uncertainty and Δt is the time uncertainty. Here, ħ is the reduced Planck constant, approximately 1.0545718 × 10^-34 J·s.

So, if we want to resolve two energy levels, we need the energy difference between them (ΔE) to be large enough so that their lifetimes (Δt1 and Δt2) are within our measurement capabilities. In other words, if the lifetimes are too short, the uncertainty in energy would be too large to distinguish the two levels clearly.

Wait, so the lifetimes given are 10^-9 seconds and 10^-8 seconds. That's pretty short! For such short lifetimes, the corresponding energy uncertainty due to the uncertainty principle would be large, right? So, to clearly resolve the two energy levels, the energy difference should be large enough to overcome this uncertainty.

Let me write this down:

ΔE ≥ ħ / (2Δt)

I remember that in quantum mechanics, the energy levels of a system are quantized, and each level has a certain lifetime. The lifetime is related to the uncertainty principle. Specifically, the Heisenberg uncertainty principle connects the uncertainty in energy (or equivalently, the energy difference between two states) and the uncertainty in time (the lifetime). 

The uncertainty principle in energy-time form is ΔE * Δt ≥ ħ/2, where ΔE is the energy difference, Δt is the lifetime, and ħ is the reduced Planck's constant. So, for two states to be clearly distinguished, the energy difference should be large enough that the uncertainty in energy is significant compared to their lifetimes.

ΔE ≥ ħ / (2Δt)

Okay, so I have this question about quantum states and their lifetimes. It says that two quantum states have energies E1 and E2 with lifetimes of 10^-9 seconds and 10^-8 seconds, respectively. The question is about determining the energy difference between these two states so that they can be clearly distinguished. The correct answer is given as 10^-8 eV, but I want to figure out why that is the case.

First, I remember that in quantum mechanics, the uncertainty principle plays a crucial role. The Heisenberg Uncertainty Principle relates the uncertainty in energy ( ΔE ) to the uncertainty in time ( Δt ). The formula is ΔE * Δt ≥ ħ/2, where ħ is the reduced Planck's constant. 

So, if we have two states with different lifetimes, the energy difference should be large enough so that the corresponding uncertainties don't overlap, making them distinguishable. That means the energy difference should be on the order of ħ divided by the characteristic time scale of the system, which in this case is the lifetimes of the states.

Let me write that down:

ΔE ≈ ħ / Δt

Given that, the lifetimes are 10^-9 seconds and 10^-8 seconds. These are very small time scales, so the corresponding energy differences should be quite large. But the answer is 10^-8 eV, which seems a bit on the smaller side. Maybe I need to compute the exact value.

ħ = 1.0545718 x 10^-34 J * s * (1 eV / 1.602176634 x 10^-19 J) ≈ ?

Let me compute that:

1.0545718 x 10^-34 / 1.602176634 x 10^-19 ≈

First, 1.0545718 / 1.602176634 ≈ 0.657

So, 0.657 x 10^(-34 + 19) = 0.657 x 10^-15 eV·s

Therefore, ħ ≈ 6.57 x 10^-16 eV·s.

Alright, so I remember that in quantum mechanics, the Heisenberg uncertainty principle is a key concept when dealing with energy and time measurements. The principle states that it's impossible to precisely know both the energy and the lifetime of a quantum state simultaneously. The more precisely you know one, the less precisely you can know the other. This uncertainty is quantified by a relation involving energy ( ΔE ) and lifetime ( τ ), with the uncertainty principle given by:

ΔE * τ ≥ ħ / 2

Where ħ is the reduced Planck constant. So, to clearly distinguish two energy levels, the energy difference between them must be large enough so that their lifetimes can be resolved.

Okay, so I have this quantum mechanics problem here about distinguishing two energy levels with their respective lifetimes. Let me try to figure this out step by step.

First, the problem says we have two quantum states with energies E1 and E2. Their lifetimes are 10^-9 seconds and 10^-8 seconds, respectively. The question is about finding the energy difference between them that would allow us to clearly distinguish these two energy levels. The answer is given as 10^-8 eV, but I need to understand why.

Hmm, I remember something about the Heisenberg uncertainty principle. It relates the uncertainty in energy and the uncertainty in time (or lifetime) of a state. The principle states that ΔE * Δt ≥ ħ/2, where ΔE is the energy uncertainty and Δt is the time uncertainty.

In this case, each state has a certain lifetime, which is the inverse of the energy difference. The lifetime τ is related to the energy difference ΔE by ΔE ≈ ħ / τ. But wait, isn't that just an approximation? Because from the Heisenberg principle, ΔE * τ ≥ ħ/2. So the minimum energy difference required to resolve two states with lifetimes τ1 and τ2 is when they satisfy ΔE * τ1 ≥ ħ/2 and ΔE * τ2 ≥ ħ/2.

Let me write that down:

For E1 with τ1 = 10^-9 s:
ΔE * 10^-9 ≥ ħ / 2

Similarly, for E2 with τ2 = 10^-8 s:
ΔE * 10^-8 ≥ ħ / 2

So, ΔE must satisfy both inequalities. To find the minimum ΔE that works for both, I need to take the maximum of the two required ΔE values.

Let's compute both:

First, ħ (h-bar) is approximately 1.0545718 × 10^-34 J·s.

But wait, the answer is in eV, so I should convert ħ to eV·s.

1 eV = 1.602176634 × 10^-19 J, so:

ħ = 1.0545718 × 10^-34 J·s * (1 eV / 1.602176634 × 10^-19 J) ≈ 6.582 × 10^-25 eV·s

So, ħ ≈ 6.582 × 10^-25 eV·s.

Now, let's compute ΔE for each state.

Starting with E1:

ΔE1 ≥ ħ / (2 * τ1) = (6.582 × 10^-25 eV·s) / (2 * 10^-9 s) ≈ (6.582 × 10^-25) / (2 × 10^-9) eV ≈ 3.291 × 10^-16 eV.

Similarly, for E2:

ΔE2 ≥ ħ / (2 * τ2) = (6.582 × 10^-25 eV·s) / (2 * 10^-8 s) ≈ (6.582 × 10^-25) / (2 × 10^-8) eV ≈ 3.291 × 10^-17 eV.

So, the required ΔE for E1 is about 3.29 × 10^-16 eV and for E2 is about 3.29 × 10^-17 eV. Since we need the energy difference to clearly distinguish both, we should take the larger one, which is 3.29 × 10^-16 eV.

Wait, but the answer is 10^-8 eV, which is much larger. That doesn't match my calculation. Did I make a mistake?

Maybe I need to think about the linewidth of the spectral lines. When you measure the energy difference, you get a line with a certain width. The linewidth is related to the lifetime of the state. The broader the linewidth, the shorter the lifetime.

The energy difference ΔE is related to the frequency difference between the two states: ΔE = hν, where ν is the frequency. The linewidth is related to the inverse of the lifetime: width ≈ 1/τ.

Therefore, to distinguish two lines, their energy difference must be larger than the sum of their linewidths.

Another way to think about it is using the concept of energy resolution. The minimum energy resolution Δ(E) needed to resolve two energy levels ΔE apart is when the width of the spectral lines is smaller than half the energy difference. Okay, so I have this problem about quantum states with different lifetimes, and I need to figure out which energy difference would allow us to clearly distinguish these two energy levels. Hmm, let me try to break this down step by step.

First, the problem states that we have two quantum states with energies E1 and E2. Their lifetimes are 10^-9 seconds and 10^-8 seconds, respectively. The goal is to clearly distinguish these two energy levels by their energy difference. The options are given, but since they aren't listed here, I'll focus on the reasoning that should lead me to the correct answer.

I remember that in quantum mechanics, the energy levels of a system are often related to their lifetimes through the uncertainty principle. Specifically, the Heisenberg uncertainty principle states that there's a fundamental limit to our ability to precisely know both the energy and the lifetime of a quantum state simultaneously. The principle can be expressed as:

ΔE * Δt ≥ ħ / 2

Where:
- ΔE is the energy uncertainty
- Δt is the time uncertainty
- ħ is the reduced Planck constant (approximately 1.0545718 × 10^-34 J·s)

So, if we know the lifetimes of the two states, we can use the uncertainty principle to find the minimum energy difference required for their energy levels to be clearly distinguished.

Let me calculate the energy difference needed for each state. Since the lifetimes are given, I can find the corresponding energy uncertainty.

For the first state with lifetime Δt1 = 10^-9 seconds:

ΔE1 ≥ ħ / (2 * Δt1)

Plugging in the numbers:

ΔE1 ≥ (1.0545718 × 10^-34 J·s) / (2 * 10^-9 s)
ΔE1 ≥ (1.0545718 × 10^-34) / (2 × 10^-9)
ΔE1 ≈ 5.2728589 × 10^-26 J

Similarly, for the second state with lifetime Δt2 = 10^-8 seconds:

ΔE2 ≥ ħ / (2 * Δt2)

ΔE2 = (1.0545718 × 10^-34) / (2 × 10^-8)
ΔE2 ≈ 5.2728589 × 10^-26 / 10 ≈ 5.2728589 × 10^-26 J

Alright, so I have this physics problem here about quantum states with different lifetimes and energy differences. Let me try to work through it step by step.

First, the problem says we have two quantum states with energies E1 and E2. Their lifetimes are 10^-9 seconds and 10^-8 seconds, respectively. We want to clearly distinguish these two energy levels. The question is asking which energy difference would allow us to do that, and the answer is supposed to be 10^-8 eV. Hmm, okay, let me think.

I remember that in quantum mechanics, the lifetimes of energy levels are related to the uncertainty principle. The Heisenberg uncertainty principle states that the product of the energy uncertainty and the lifetime uncertainty is on the order of Planck's constant. Mathematically, that's ΔE * Δt ≈ ħ, where ΔE is the energy uncertainty and Δt is the time uncertainty.

So, if we have a state with energy E and lifetime τ, the uncertainty in energy ΔE would be roughly ħ / τ. That makes sense because a shorter lifetime (smaller τ) means a larger uncertainty in energy, and vice versa.

Now, since we want to clearly distinguish two energy levels, we need their energy difference to be large enough that their lifetimes don't overlap too much. In other words, the energy difference should be such that ΔE is larger than the uncertainty in energy for each state. Otherwise, their lifetimes (and hence their energies) might blur together.

Let me write down the uncertainty relation for each state:

For E1: ΔE ≈ ħ / τ1

For E2: ΔE ≈ ħ / τ2

But since we want to resolve the two states, the energy difference between them should be larger than the uncertainty in each individual energy. So, the minimum energy difference required would be approximately the sum of the individual energy uncertainties, right? That is, ΔE_min ≈ ΔE1 + ΔE2.

Substituting the values:

ΔE_min ≈ ħ / τ1 + ħ / τ2

Let me compute that. The Planck constant ħ is approximately 1.0545718 × 10^-34 J·s. The lifetimes are given in seconds:

τ1 = 10^-9 s and τ2 = 10^-8 s.

So,

ΔE_min ≈ (1.0545718 × 10^-34 J·s) / (10^-9 s) + (1.0545718 × 10^-34 J·s) / (10^-8 s)

Calculating each term:

First term: 1.0545718 × 10^-34 / 10^-9 = 1.0545718 × 10^-25 J

Second term: 1.0545718 × 10^-34 / 10^-8 = 1.0545718 × 10^-26 J

Adding them together:

1.0545718 × 10^-25 + 1.0545718 × 10^-26 ≈ 1.2091356 × 10^-25 J

Hmm, that's in joules. Let me convert that to electron volts because the answer is given in eV. I remember that 1 eV is approximately 1.602176634 × 10^-19 J.

So, to convert from joules to eV, we divide by the value of 1 eV in joules:

ΔE_min (eV) ≈ 1.2091356 × 10^-25 J / 1.602176634 × 10^-19 J/eV

Calculating that:

1.2091356 / 1.602176634 ≈ 0.754

Then, 10^-25 / 10^-19 = 10^-6

So, ΔE_min ≈ 0.754 × 10^-6 eV ≈ 7.54 × 10^-7 eV

Wait, that's about 7.54 × 10^-7 eV. But the answer given is 10^-8 eV. Hmm, that's lower than what I calculated. So, am I missing something here?

Let me think again. Maybe I overestimated the energy difference needed. The uncertainty principle gives a lower bound on the energy uncertainty, but perhaps for resolving two states, we need only that the energy difference between them is larger than the uncertainty in each individual state's energy.
